GRI 3.0 mechanism (Smith, 1999) for methane and air mixtu-
res. The results are shown in figure 1.

An original methodology is proposed to calculate the cri-
tical extinction factor, also as a function of the mixture equi-
valence ratio and for different pressure and temperature va-
lues upstream of the flame.

It is assumed that flame extinction occurs where the re-
action has been sufficiently disturbed by turbulence, thus,

142  n   Boletim Técnico da Petrobras, Rio de Janeiro, v. 54, n. 1/2, p. 139-147, abr./ago. 2011



 Structure of the  
reactive flow

This section describes the influence of the model cons-
tant Cg (equation 5) on the reactive flow field structure re-
garding the equivalence ratio and reaction progress variable 
evolution.  

Equivalence ratio field 

The equivalence ratio field is shown in figure 6 for di-
fferent values of Cg = 1, 10 and 100. In the flow field do -
wnstream of the pilot stage in the center of the combustion 
chamber, the influence of Cg is not perceptible. However, the 
mixing flow field downstream of stages A&B is influenced by 
the choice of Cg. When Cg increases a larger segregation be-
tween fuel and air is observed. This indicates the combustion 
process at the stages A&B influences the scalar mixing field.  

Progress variable field (c)
The influence of this progress variable field with respect 

to Cg is shown in figure 7. As indicated, the “c” field is direc-
tly linked to the temperature. c = 0 represents the fresh un-
burned gases and c = 1 the fully burned state.    

When Cg = 1, combustion of the stages A&B begins near 
the combustion chamber exit, downstream of the cooling ori-
fices, which is incorrect. Furthermore, the incomplete com-

is premixed with fuel, and then enters the combustion cham-
ber where the fuel is injected and the reaction occurs.

Inside the chamber, fuel is injected through eight orifices 
inclined with respect to the chamber central axis, and  four 
other orifices located in eight peripheral masts, making 40 in-
jection points overall. The pilot stage, which is responsible for 
combustion stabilization, is separated from stages A&B by a 
conical surface.

Once the domain representation in CAD geometry was 
made, it was necessary to discretisize this domain into small 
volumes for the CFD calculation. A representation of the used 
mesh is shown in figure 5.

Boundary Conditions 
This study considers the combustion chamber operation 

to be at base load conditions (98% load). The mass flow ra-
tes, temperature and operating pressure in the different in-
jection stages are prescribed according to table 1.

The nonreactive case turbulent field was used as the ini-
tial condition for the reactive cases. The boundary conditions 
consisted of: adiabatic walls, no slip surfaces, temperature, 
composition, mass flow rate and turbulent quantities at the 
inflow boundaries, and with pressure at the exit. A symmetry 
considering 1/4 sector was used for the combustion cham-
ber domain.

Flame stabilization  
mechanism 

The turbulent flame speed ST  and the ratio ?/ ?cr for diffe-
rent values of Cg, are shown in figures 9 and 10. The former 
expresses the possibility the flame propagates with a speed 
that must stabilize the local flow velocity for the flame stabi-
lity, the latter shows whether the flame extinction by turbu-
lence take place or not.

When Cg = 1, small values of S T  occur at the combus-
tion chamber periphery and downstream of the pilot stage, 
as shown in figure 10, when compared to those computed

for Cg = 10 or 100. Therefore, it is more likely for a turbulent 
flame to be found in cases where Cg = 10 or 100 than with 
Cg = 1. The extreme influence of the choice of C g on ?/?cr  
may be confirmed for the case with Cg = 100, where the tur -
bulent flame could be stabilized almost anywhere within the 
combustion chamber. The opposite is observed for Cg = 1, 
and the case of Cg = 10 is an intermediate situation where 
the flow field alters quenching and stabilizing regions. 

                     

Conclusion
The study presented the parametrical analysis results of 

the influence of the turbulent combustion model parameter 
choice on the gas turbine combustion chamber computed 
flow field. Also a new expression for this model parameter, 
which represents flame extinction by turbulence, was propo-
sed as a function of the equivalence ratio. 

The results facilitate the evaluation and establishment of 
a plausible value for Cg as approximately 10. The exact value 

should be identified by comparing the model to representati-
ve experiments, such as those of Besson et al. (2000).
